   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Lyme disease
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_Disease&amp;redirect=no" title="Lyme Disease">

      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

       <th scope="row">

        </a>

       <td>
        <a class="external text" href="//www.nlm.nih.gov/cgi/mesh/2015/MB_cgi?field=uid&amp;term=D008193" rel="nofollow">

        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <b>
       Lyme disease
      </b>
      , also known as
      <b>
       Lyme borreliosis
      </b>
      , is an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">
       infectious disease
      </a>
      caused by
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Borrelia" title="Borrelia">
        Borrelia
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">
       type
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most common sign of infection is an expanding area of redness, known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythema_migrans" title="Erythema migrans">
       erythema migrans
      </a>
      , that begins at the site of a tick bite about a week after it has occurred. The rash is typically neither itchy nor painful. About 25% of people do not develop a rash. Other early symptoms may include
      <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">
       fever
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">
       headache
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">
       feeling tired
      </a>
      . If untreated, symptoms may include
      <a href="/wiki/Bell%27s_palsy" title="Bell's palsy">
       loss of the ability to move one or both sides of the face
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">
       joint pains
      </a>
      , severe headaches with neck stiffness, or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_palpitation" title="Heart palpitation">
       heart palpitations
      </a>
      , among others. Months to years later, repeated episodes of joint pain and swelling may occur. Occasionally, people develop shooting pains or tingling in their arms and legs. Despite appropriate treatment, about 10 to 20% of people also develop joint pains, have problems with memory, and feel tired much of the time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013S_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC2013S-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lyme disease is transmitted to humans by the bite of infected
      <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">
       ticks
      </a>
      of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes" title="Ixodes">
        Ixodes
       </a>
      </i>
      genus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baron_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Baron-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Usually, the tick must be attached for 36 to 48 hours before the bacteria can spread.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013T_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC2013T-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In North America, the only bacterium involved is
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Borrelia_burgdorferi" title="Borrelia burgdorferi">
        Borrelia burgdorferi
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensu_stricto" title="Sensu stricto">
        sensu stricto
       </a>
      </i>
      , while in Europe and Asia, the bacteria
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Borrelia_afzelii" title="Borrelia afzelii">
        Borrelia afzelii
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Borrelia_garinii" title="Borrelia garinii">
        Borrelia garinii
       </a>
      </i>
      are also causes of the disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The disease does not appear to be transmissible between people, by other animals, or through food.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013T_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC2013T-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Diagnosis is based upon a combination of symptoms, history of tick exposure, and possibly testing for specific
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">
       antibodies
      </a>
      in the blood.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Blood tests are often negative in the early stages of the disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Testing of individual ticks is not typically useful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Prevention includes efforts to prevent tick bites such as by wearing long pants and using
      <a href="/wiki/DEET" title="DEET">
       DEET
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using
      <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">
       pesticides
      </a>
      to reduce tick numbers may also be effective.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ticks can be removed using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tweezer" title="Tweezer">
       tweezers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If the removed tick was full of blood, a single dose of
      <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">
       doxycycline
      </a>
      may be used to prevent development of infection, but is not generally recommended since development of infection is rare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If an infection develops, a number of antibiotics are effective, including
      <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">
       doxycycline
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin" title="Amoxicillin">
       amoxicillin
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Cefuroxime" title="Cefuroxime">
       cefuroxime
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Treatment is usually for two or three weeks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some people develop a fever and muscle and joint pains from treatment which may last for one or two days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In those who develop persistent symptoms, long-term antibiotic therapy has not been found to be useful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013S_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC2013S-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lyme disease is the most common disease spread by ticks in the
      <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">
       Northern Hemisphere
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is estimated to affect 300,000 people a year in the United States and 65,000 people a year in Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Infections are most common in the spring and early summer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lyme disease was diagnosed as a separate condition for the first time in 1975 in
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Lyme,_Connecticut" title="Old Lyme, Connecticut">
       Old Lyme, Connecticut
      </a>
      (it was originally mistaken for
      <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_idiopathic_arthritis" title="Juvenile idiopathic arthritis">
       juvenile rheumatoid arthritis
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The bacterium involved was first described in 1981 by
      <a href="/wiki/Willy_Burgdorfer" title="Willy Burgdorfer">
       Willy Burgdorfer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tele2014_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tele2014-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chronic symptoms are well described and are known as post-treatment Lyme disease syndrome, although it is often called
      <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease_controversy" title="Lyme disease controversy">
       chronic Lyme disease
      </a>
      . Some healthcare providers claim that it is due to ongoing infection; however, this is not believed to be true.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2014PT_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC2014PT-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A previous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyme_vaccine" title="Lyme vaccine">
       vaccine
      </a>
      is no longer available. Research is ongoing to develop new vaccines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="toclimit-3">

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">
       Signs and symptoms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3880" data-file-width="2608" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg/220px-Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg/330px-Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg/440px-Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Erythema_migrans_-_erythematous_rash_in_Lyme_disease_-_PHIL_9875.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        This "classic" bull's-eye rash is also called erythema migrans. A rash caused by Lyme does not always look like this. Around 20% to 30% of persons who are infected with Lyme disease may have no rash.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC-Signs-Symptoms-April-2011_14-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-CDC-Signs-Symptoms-April-2011-14">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          14
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WebMD-October-2012_15-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-WebMD-October-2012-15">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          15
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lymerash.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/Lymerash.jpg/220px-Lymerash.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/Lymerash.jpg/330px-Lymerash.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/Lymerash.jpg/440px-Lymerash.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lymerash.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Raised, red borders around indurated central portion
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Lyme disease can affect multiple body systems and produce a broad range of symptoms. Not all patients with Lyme disease have all symptoms, and many of the symptoms are not specific to Lyme disease, but can occur with other diseases, as well. The
      <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">
       incubation period
      </a>
      from infection to the onset of symptoms is usually one to two weeks, but can be much shorter (days), or much longer (months to years).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lymedisease_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lymedisease-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Symptoms most often occur from May to September, because the nymphal stage of the tick is responsible for most cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lymedisease_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lymedisease-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic" title="Asymptomatic">
       Asymptomatic
      </a>
      infection exists, but occurs in less than 7% of infected individuals in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12905137_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid12905137-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Asymptomatic infection may be much more common among those infected in Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23319969_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid23319969-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9556169_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9556169-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_localized_infection">
       Early localized infection
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early localized infection">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Early localized infection can occur when the infection has not yet spread throughout the body. Only the site where the infection has first come into contact with the skin is affected. The classic sign of early local infection with Lyme disease is a circular, outwardly expanding rash called
      <a href="/wiki/Erythema_chronicum_migrans" title="Erythema chronicum migrans">
       erythema chronicum migrans
      </a>
      (EM), which occurs at the site of the tick bite three to 30 days after the tick bite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed_ophth_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-emed_ophth-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-159991-6_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-159991-6-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rash is red, and may be warm, but is generally painless. Classically, the innermost portion remains dark red and becomes
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Induration" title="Induration">
       indurated
      </a>
      (is thicker and firmer), the outer edge remains red, and the portion in between clears, giving the appearance of a
      <a href="/wiki/Bullseye_(target)" title="Bullseye (target)">
       bull's eye
      </a>
      . However, partial clearing is uncommon, and the bull's-eye pattern more often involves central redness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11900494_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11900494-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The EM rash associated with early infection is found in about 80% of patients
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-159991-6_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-159991-6-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and can have a range of appearances including the classic target bull's-eye lesion and nontarget appearing lesions. The 20% without the EM and the nontarget lesions can often cause misidentification of Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aucott_2012_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-aucott_2012-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Affected individuals can also experience
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flu-like_symptoms" title="Flu-like symptoms">
       flu-like symptoms
      </a>
      , such as
      <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">
       headache
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_soreness" title="Muscle soreness">
       muscle soreness
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">
       fever
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">
       malaise
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14987414_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid14987414-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lyme disease can progress to later stages even in patients who do not develop a rash.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23319969_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid23319969-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12543291_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid12543291-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_disseminated_infection">
       Early disseminated infection
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early disseminated infection">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Within days to weeks after the onset of local infection, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Borrelia" title="Borrelia">
        Borrelia
       </a>
      </i>
      bacteria may begin to spread through the bloodstream. EM may develop at sites across the body that bear no relation to the original tick bite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18452799_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18452799-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another skin condition, apparently absent in North American patients, but found in Europe, is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borrelial_lymphocytoma" title="Borrelial lymphocytoma">
       borrelial lymphocytoma
      </a>
      , a purplish lump that develops on the ear lobe, nipple, or
      <a href="/wiki/Scrotum" title="Scrotum">
       scrotum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18452805_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18452805-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other discrete symptoms include migrating pain in muscles, joints, and tendons, and dizziness.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1109" data-file-width="1345" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg/220px-Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg/330px-Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg/440px-Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borrelial_lymphocytoma.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borrelial_lymphocytoma" title="Borrelial lymphocytoma">
         Borrelial lymphocytoma
        </a>
        on the cheek (very uncommon)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Various acute neurological problems, termed
      <a href="/wiki/Neuroborreliosis" title="Neuroborreliosis">
       neuroborreliosis
      </a>
      , appear in 1015% of untreated patients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14987414_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid14987414-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halperin2008_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Halperin2008-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These include
      <a href="/wiki/Bell%27s_palsy" title="Bell's palsy">
       facial palsy
      </a>
      , which is the loss of muscle tone on one or both sides of the face, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
       meningitis
      </a>
      , which involves severe headaches,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuchal_rigidity" title="Nuchal rigidity">
       neck stiffness
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Photophobia" title="Photophobia">
       sensitivity to light
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiculoneuritis" title="Radiculoneuritis">
       Inflammation of the spinal cord's nerve roots
      </a>
      can cause shooting pains that may interfere with sleep, as well as abnormal skin sensations. Mild
      <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">
       encephalitis
      </a>
      may lead to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_loss" title="Memory loss">
       memory loss
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleep_disturbances" title="Sleep disturbances">
       sleep disturbances
      </a>
      , or mood changes. In addition, some
      <a href="/wiki/Case_report" title="Case report">
       case reports
      </a>
      have described altered mental status as the only symptom seen in a few cases of early neuroborreliosis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chabria_2007_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chabria_2007-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The disease may adversely impact the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_conduction_system" title="Heart conduction system">
       heart's electrical conduction system
      </a>
      and can cause
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrhythmias" title="Arrhythmias">
       abnormal heart rhythms
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_block" title="Atrioventricular block">
       atrioventricular block
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanek2012_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stanek2012-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Late_disseminated_infection">
       <span id="Bannwarth_syndrome">
       </span>
       Late disseminated infection
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Late disseminated infection">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      After several months, untreated or inadequately treated patients may go on to develop severe and chronic symptoms that affect many parts of the body, including the brain, nerves, eyes, joints, and heart. Many disabling symptoms can occur, including permanent
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraparesis" title="Paraparesis">
       impairment of motor or sensory function
      </a>
      of the lower extremities in extreme cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23319969_18-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid23319969-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The associated nerve pain radiating out from the spine is termed Bannwarth syndrome,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19562015_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19562015-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      named after
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Bannwarth" title="Alfred Bannwarth">
       Alfred Bannwarth
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The late disseminated stage is where the infection has fully spread throughout the body. Chronic neurologic symptoms occur in up to 5% of untreated patients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14987414_24-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid14987414-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Polyneuropathy" title="Polyneuropathy">
       polyneuropathy
      </a>
      that involves shooting pains, numbness, and tingling in the hands or feet may develop. A neurologic syndrome called Lyme encephalopathy is associated with subtle cognitive problems, such as difficulties with concentration and short-term memory. These patients may also experience fatigue.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shadick_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shadick-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other problems, however, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">
       depression
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fibromyalgia" title="Fibromyalgia">
       fibromyalgia
      </a>
      , are no more common in people with Lyme disease than in the general population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shadick_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shadick-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seltzer_2000_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seltzer_2000-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chronic
      <a href="/wiki/Encephalomyelitis" title="Encephalomyelitis">
       encephalomyelitis
      </a>
      , which may be progressive, can involve cognitive impairment, brain fog, migraines, balance issues weakness in the legs, awkward gait, facial palsy, bladder problems,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertigo_(medical)" title="Vertigo (medical)">
       vertigo
      </a>
      , and back pain. In rare cases, untreated Lyme disease may cause
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_psychosis" title="Frank psychosis">
       frank psychosis
      </a>
      , which has been misdiagnosed as
      <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">
       schizophrenia
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">
       bipolar disorder
      </a>
      . Panic attacks and anxiety can occur; also, delusional behavior may be seen, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatoform" title="Somatoform">
       somatoform
      </a>
      delusions, sometimes accompanied by a
      <a href="/wiki/Depersonalization" title="Depersonalization">
       depersonalization
      </a>
      or derealization syndrome, where the patients begin to feel detached from themselves or from reality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fallon_1994_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fallon_1994-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hess_1999_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hess_1999-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lyme arthritis usually affects the knees.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18452802_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18452802-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a minority of patients, arthritis can occur in other joints, including the ankles, elbows, wrists, hips, and shoulders. Pain is often mild or moderate, usually with swelling at the involved joint.
      <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_cyst" title="Baker's cyst">
       Baker's cysts
      </a>
      may form and rupture. In some cases, joint erosion occurs.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Acrodermatitis_chronica_atrophicans" title="Acrodermatitis chronica atrophicans">
        Acrodermatitis chronica atrophicans
       </a>
      </i>
      (ACA) is a chronic skin disorder observed primarily in Europe among the elderly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18452805_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18452805-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ACA begins as a reddish-blue patch of discolored skin, often on the backs of the hands or feet. The lesion slowly atrophies over several weeks or months, with the skin becoming first thin and wrinkled and then, if untreated, completely dry and hairless.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mullegger_2004_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mullegger_2004-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">
       Cause
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cause">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease_microbiology" title="Lyme disease microbiology">
       Lyme disease microbiology
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="354" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg/250px-Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg/375px-Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg/500px-Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deer_Tick_life_cycle.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Deer tick life cycle
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="346" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg/220px-Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg/330px-Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/15/Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borrelia_burgdorferi-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Borrelia" title="Borrelia">
          Borrelia
         </a>
        </i>
        bacteria, the causative agent of Lyme disease, magnified
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ixodes_scapularis.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1121" data-file-width="1415" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ixodes_scapularis.png/220px-Ixodes_scapularis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ixodes_scapularis.png/330px-Ixodes_scapularis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ixodes_scapularis.png/440px-Ixodes_scapularis.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ixodes_scapularis.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_scapularis" title="Ixodes scapularis">
          Ixodes scapularis
         </a>
        </i>
        , the primary vector of Lyme disease in eastern North America
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Lyme disease is caused by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirochetal" title="Spirochetal">
       spirochetal
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      from the
      <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">
       genus
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Borrelia" title="Borrelia">
        Borrelia
       </a>
      </i>
      . Spirochetes are surrounded by
      <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">
       peptidoglycan
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagella" title="Flagella">
       flagella
      </a>
      , along with an outer membrane similar to other Gram-negative bacteria. Because of their double-membrane envelope,
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      bacteria are often mistakenly described as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram_negative" title="Gram negative">
       Gram negative
      </a>
      despite the considerable differences in their envelope components from Gram-negative bacteria.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-samuels_2010_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-samuels_2010-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Lyme-related
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      species are collectively known as
      <i>
       Borrelia burgdorferi
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensu_lato" title="Sensu lato">
        sensu lato
       </a>
      </i>
      , and show a great deal of
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">
       genetic diversity
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      is made up of 18 closely related species, but only three clearly cause Lyme disease:
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensu_stricto" title="Sensu stricto">
        sensu stricto
       </a>
      </i>
      (predominant in
      <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">
       North America
      </a>
      , but also present in
      <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
       Europe
      </a>
      ),
      <i>
       B. afzelii
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       B. garinii
      </i>
      (both predominant in
      <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">
       Eurasia
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanek2011_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stanek2011-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some studies have also proposed
      <i>
       B. bissettii
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       B. valaisiana
      </i>
      may sometimes infect humans, but these species do not seem to be important causes of disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18454594_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18454594-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18650352_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18650352-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Weather_and_climate_effects_on_Lyme_disease_exposure" title="Weather and climate effects on Lyme disease exposure">
       Weather and climate effects on Lyme disease exposure
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">
       Transmission
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Transmission">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Lyme disease is classified as a
      <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">
       zoonosis
      </a>
      , as it is transmitted to humans from a
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">
       natural reservoir
      </a>
      among rodents by
      <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">
       ticks
      </a>
      that feed on both sets of
      <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">
       hosts
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18452798_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18452798-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hard-bodied ticks of the genus
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes" title="Ixodes">
        Ixodes
       </a>
      </i>
      are the main
      <a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">
       vectors
      </a>
      of Lyme disease (also the vector for
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Babesia" title="Babesia">
        Babesia
       </a>
      </i>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sherris-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most infections are caused by ticks in the
      <a href="/wiki/Nymph_(biology)" title="Nymph (biology)">
       nymphal stage
      </a>
      , as they are very small and may feed for long periods of time undetected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18452798_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18452798-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Larval ticks are very rarely infected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15117014_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15117014-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although deer are the preferred hosts of deer ticks, and the size of the tick population parallels that of the deer population, ticks cannot acquire Lyme disease spirochetes from deer. Rather, deer ticks acquire
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      microbes from infected rodents, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/White-footed_mouse" title="White-footed mouse">
       white-footed mouse
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Peromyscus leucopus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westport_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Westport-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Within the tick midgut, the
      <i>
       Borrelia'
      </i>
      s outer surface protein A (OspA) binds to the tick receptor for OspA, known as TROSPA. When the tick feeds, the
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      downregulates OspA and upregulates OspC, another surface protein. After the bacteria migrate from the midgut to the salivary glands, OspC binds to Salp15, a tick salivary protein that appears to have immunosuppressive effects that enhance infection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hovius_2007_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hovius_2007-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Successful infection of the mammalian host depends on bacterial expression of OspC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15085185_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15085185-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tick bites often go unnoticed because of the small size of the tick in its nymphal stage, as well as tick secretions that prevent the host from feeling any itch or pain from the bite. However, transmission is quite rare, with only about 1% of recognized tick bites resulting in Lyme disease. Transmission may occur within 24 hours of the tick bite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11450660_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11450660-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Europe, the vector is
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_ricinus" title="Ixodes ricinus">
        Ixodes ricinus
       </a>
      </i>
      , which is also called the sheep tick or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castor_oil_plant" title="Castor oil plant">
       castor bean
      </a>
      tick.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_1997_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-de_1997-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In China,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_persulcatus" title="Ixodes persulcatus">
        Ixodes persulcatus
       </a>
      </i>
      (the taiga tick) is probably the most important vector.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun_2003_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sun_2003-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In North America, the black-legged tick or deer tick (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_scapularis" title="Ixodes scapularis">
        Ixodes scapularis
       </a>
      </i>
      ) is the main vector on the East Coast.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15117014_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15117014-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The lone star tick (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amblyomma_americanum" title="Amblyomma americanum">
        Amblyomma americanum
       </a>
      </i>
      ), which is found throughout the
      <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">
       Southeastern United States
      </a>
      as far west as
      <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
       Texas
      </a>
      , is unlikely to transmit the Lyme disease
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirochete" title="Spirochete">
       spirochetes
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ledin_2005_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ledin_2005-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though it may be implicated in a related syndrome called
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_tick-associated_rash_illness" title="Southern tick-associated rash illness">
       southern tick-associated rash illness
      </a>
      , which resembles a mild form of Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masters2008_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Masters2008-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On the
      <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">
       West Coast of the United States
      </a>
      , the main vector is the western black-legged tick (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_pacificus" title="Ixodes pacificus">
        Ixodes pacificus
       </a>
      </i>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clark-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The tendency of this tick species to feed predominantly on host species such as lizards that are resistant to
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      infection appears to diminish transmission of Lyme disease in the West.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15709249_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15709249-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16995383_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16995383-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Transmission across the
      <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">
       placenta
      </a>
      during pregnancy has not been demonstrated, and no consistent pattern of
      <a href="/wiki/Teratology" title="Teratology">
       teratogenicity
      </a>
      or specific "congenital Lyme borreliosis" has been identified. As with a number of other spirochetal diseases, adverse pregnancy outcomes are possible with untreated infection; prompt treatment with antibiotics reduces or eliminates this risk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh2007_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Walsh2007-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakos2010_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lakos2010-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While Lyme spirochetes have been found in
      <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">
       insects
      </a>
      , as well as ticks,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnarelli_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Magnarelli-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      reports of actual infectious transmission appear to be rare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luger_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Luger-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lyme spirochete DNA has been found in semen
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bach_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bach-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and breast milk,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but transmission has not been known to take place through sexual contact.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steere_2003_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steere_2003-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the CDC, live spirochetes have not been found in breast milk, urine, or semen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC-FAQ_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC-FAQ-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, more recent studies published in 2014, suggest a link might exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tick-borne_coinfections">
       Tick-borne coinfections
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tick-borne coinfections">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Ticks that transmit
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      to humans can also carry and transmit several other parasites, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Theileria_microti" title="Theileria microti">
        Theileria microti
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Anaplasma_phagocytophilum" title="Anaplasma phagocytophilum">
        Anaplasma phagocytophilum
       </a>
      </i>
      , which cause the diseases
      <a href="/wiki/Babesiosis" title="Babesiosis">
       babesiosis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Human_granulocytic_anaplasmosis" title="Human granulocytic anaplasmosis">
       human granulocytic anaplasmosis
      </a>
      (HGA), respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17041141_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17041141-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Among early Lyme disease patients, depending on their location, 212% will also have HGA and 240% will have babesiosis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16807416_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16807416-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ticks in certain regions, including the lands along the eastern Baltic Sea, also transmit
      <a href="/wiki/Tick-borne_encephalitis" title="Tick-borne encephalitis">
       tick-borne encephalitis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindgren_2001_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lindgren_2001-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Coinfections complicate Lyme symptoms, especially diagnosis and treatment. It is possible for a tick to carry and transmit one of the coinfections and not
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      , making diagnosis difficult and often elusive. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control" title="Centers for Disease Control">
       Centers for Disease Control
      </a>
      studied 100 ticks in rural
      <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
       New Jersey
      </a>
      , and found 55% of the ticks were infected with at least one of the pathogens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9452402_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9452402-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">
       Pathophysiology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      can spread throughout the body during the course of the disease, and has been found in the skin, heart, joints, peripheral nervous system, and central nervous system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15085185_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15085185-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17509489_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17509489-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of the signs and symptoms of Lyme disease are a consequence of the immune response to the spirochete in those tissues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14987414_24-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid14987414-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      is injected into the skin by the bite of an infected
      <i>
       Ixodes
      </i>
      tick. Tick saliva, which accompanies the spirochete into the skin during the feeding process, contains substances that disrupt the immune response at the site of the bite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16698304_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16698304-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This provides a protective environment where the spirochete can establish infection. The spirochetes multiply and migrate outward within the
      <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">
       dermis
      </a>
      . The host inflammatory response to the bacteria in the skin causes the characteristic circular EM lesion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15085185_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15085185-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrophils" title="Neutrophils">
       Neutrophils
      </a>
      , however, which are necessary to eliminate the spirochetes from the skin, fail to appear in the developing EM lesion. This allows the bacteria to survive and eventually spread throughout the body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17404293_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17404293-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Days to weeks following the tick bite, the spirochetes spread via the bloodstream to joints, heart, nervous system, and distant skin sites, where their presence gives rise to the variety of symptoms of disseminated disease. The spread of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      is aided by the attachment of the host protease
      <a href="/wiki/Plasmin" title="Plasmin">
       plasmin
      </a>
      to the surface of the spirochete.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9215633_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9215633-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      If untreated, the bacteria may persist in the body for months or even years, despite the production of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      antibodies by the immune system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11450660_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11450660-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The spirochetes may avoid the immune response by decreasing expression of surface proteins that are targeted by antibodies,
      <a href="/wiki/Antigenic_variation" title="Antigenic variation">
       antigenic variation
      </a>
      of the VlsE surface protein, inactivating key immune components such as
      <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">
       complement
      </a>
      , and hiding in the
      <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">
       extracellular matrix
      </a>
      , which may interfere with the function of immune factors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18097481_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18097481-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17600717_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17600717-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the brain,
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      may induce
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrocytes" title="Astrocytes">
       astrocytes
      </a>
      to undergo astrogliosis (proliferation followed by
      <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">
       apoptosis
      </a>
      ), which may contribute to neurodysfunction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12938230_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid12938230-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The spirochetes may also induce host cells to secrete
      <a href="/wiki/Quinolinic_acid" title="Quinolinic acid">
       quinolinic acid
      </a>
      , which stimulates the
      <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">
       NMDA receptor
      </a>
      on nerve cells, which may account for the
      <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">
       fatigue
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">
       malaise
      </a>
      observed with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyme_encephalopathy" title="Lyme encephalopathy">
       Lyme encephalopathy
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1531156_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid1531156-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, diffuse white matter pathology during Lyme encephalopathy may disrupt
      <a href="/wiki/Grey_matter" title="Grey matter">
       grey matter
      </a>
      connections, and could account for deficits in attention, memory, visuospatial ability, complex cognition, and emotional status. White matter disease may have a greater potential for recovery than gray matter disease, perhaps because neuronal loss is less common. Resolution of MRI white matter hyperintensities after antibiotic treatment has been observed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fallon_2003_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fallon_2003-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">
       Tryptophan
      </a>
      , a precursor to
      <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">
       serotonin
      </a>
      , appears to be reduced within the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      in a number of infectious diseases that affect the brain, including Lyme.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasse_1994_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gasse_1994-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Researchers are investigating if this neurohormone secretion is the cause of
      <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychiatry" title="Neuropsychiatry">
       neuropsychiatric
      </a>
      disorders developing in some patients with borreliosis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zajkowska_2006_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Zajkowska_2006-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Immunological_studies">
       Immunological studies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Immunological studies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Exposure to the
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      bacterium during Lyme disease possibly causes a long-lived and damaging
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflammatory_response" title="Inflammatory response">
       inflammatory response
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ercolini_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ercolini-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a form of pathogen-induced
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autoimmune" title="Autoimmune">
       autoimmune
      </a>
      disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Singh-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The production of this reaction might be due to a form of
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mimicry" title="Molecular mimicry">
       molecular mimicry
      </a>
      , where
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      avoids being killed by the immune system by resembling normal parts of the body's tissues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oldstone_1998_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Oldstone_1998-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raveche_2005_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raveche_2005-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chronic symptoms from an autoimmune reaction could explain why some symptoms persist even after the spirochetes have been eliminated from the body. This hypothesis may explain why chronic arthritis persists after antibiotic therapy, similar to
      <a href="/wiki/Rheumatic_fever" title="Rheumatic fever">
       rheumatic fever
      </a>
      , but its wider application is controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinstein_2002_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weinstein_2002-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bolz_2004_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bolz_2004-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Persistence">
       Persistence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Persistence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The National Institute of Health has supported research into bacterial resistance which has demonstrated persistence after antibiotic therapy in several animal models, including mice and primates. However, it was not possible to culture these bacteria and it is not known if they are infectious, or if they contribute to symptom persistence post-treatment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">
       Diagnosis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Lyme disease is
      <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">
       diagnosed
      </a>
      clinically based on symptoms, objective physical findings (such as EM, facial palsy, or arthritis), or a history of possible exposure to infected ticks, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Serology" title="Serology">
       serological blood tests
      </a>
      . The EM rash is not always a bull's eye, i.e., it can be solid red. When making a diagnosis of Lyme disease, health care providers should consider other diseases that may cause similar illnesses. Not all patients infected with Lyme disease develop the characteristic bull's-eye rash, and many may not recall a tick bite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wormser_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wormser-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Because of the difficulty in
      <a href="/wiki/Microbiological_culture" title="Microbiological culture">
       culturing
      </a>
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      bacteria in the laboratory, diagnosis of Lyme disease is typically based on the clinical exam findings and a history of exposure to
      <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">
       endemic
      </a>
      Lyme areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sherris-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The EM rash, which does not occur in all cases, is considered sufficient to establish a diagnosis of Lyme disease even when serologic blood tests are negative.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofmann_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hofmann-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Serological testing can be used to support a clinically suspected case, but is not diagnostic by itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_43-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sherris-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Diagnosis of late-stage Lyme disease is often complicated by a multifaceted appearance and nonspecific symptoms, prompting one reviewer to call Lyme the new "great imitator".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pachner_1989_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pachner_1989-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lyme disease may be misdiagnosed as
      <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">
       multiple sclerosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">
       rheumatoid arthritis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fibromyalgia" title="Fibromyalgia">
       fibromyalgia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chronic_fatigue_syndrome" title="Chronic fatigue syndrome">
       chronic fatigue syndrome
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Systemic_lupus_erythematosus" title="Systemic lupus erythematosus">
       lupus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">
       Crohn's disease
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">
       HIV
      </a>
      , or other autoimmune and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurodegenerative" title="Neurodegenerative">
       neurodegenerative
      </a>
      diseases. As all patients with later-stage infection will have a positive antibody test, simple blood tests can exclude Lyme disease as a possible cause of the patients' symptoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branda_541.E2.80.937_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Branda_541.E2.80.937-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_testing">
       Laboratory testing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Laboratory testing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Several forms of laboratory testing for Lyme disease are available, some of which have not been adequately validated. The most widely used tests are
      <a href="/wiki/Serology" title="Serology">
       serologies
      </a>
      , which measure levels of specific antibodies in a patient's blood. These tests may be negative in early infection, as the body may not have produced a significant quantity of antibodies, but they are considered a reliable aid in the diagnosis of later stages of Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc-diagnosis_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc-diagnosis-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Serologic tests for Lyme disease are of limited use in people lacking objective signs of Lyme disease because of false positive results and cost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idsa_guideline_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-idsa_guideline-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The serological laboratory tests most widely available and employed are the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_blot" title="Western blot">
       Western blot
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/ELISA" title="ELISA">
       ELISA
      </a>
      . A two-tiered protocol is recommended by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention: the
      <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">
       sensitive
      </a>
      ELISA test is performed first, and if it is positive or equivocal, then the more
      <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">
       specific
      </a>
      Western blot is run.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilske_2005_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wilske_2005-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The reliability of testing in diagnosis remains controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_43-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sherris-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies show the Western blot
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgM" title="IgM">
       IgM
      </a>
      has a specificity of 9496% for patients with clinical symptoms of early Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engstrom_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Engstrom-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sivak_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sivak-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The initial ELISA test has a sensitivity of about 70%, and in two-tiered testing, the overall sensitivity is only 64%, although this rises to 100% in the subset of people with disseminated symptoms, such as arthritis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steere_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steere-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Erroneous test results have been widely reported in both early and late stages of the disease, and can be caused by several factors, including antibody cross-reactions from other infections, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epstein-Barr_virus" title="Epstein-Barr virus">
       Epstein-Barr virus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cytomegalovirus" title="Cytomegalovirus">
       cytomegalovirus
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gossens_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gossens-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">
       herpes simplex virus
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strasfeld_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Strasfeld-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The overall rate of false positives is low, only about 1 to 3%, in comparison to a false-negative rate of up to 36% in the early stages of infection using two-tiered testing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steere_97-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steere-97">
