        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">
       Polymerase chain reaction
      </a>
      (PCR) tests for Lyme disease have also been developed to detect the genetic material (
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      ) of the Lyme disease spirochete. PCR tests are susceptible to
      <a href="/wiki/Type_I_and_type_II_errors" title="Type I and type II errors">
       false positive
      </a>
      results from poor laboratory technique.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11438915_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11438915-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even when properly performed, PCR often shows false negative results with blood and
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">
       cerebrospinal fluid
      </a>
      specimens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16020686_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16020686-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hence, PCR is not widely performed for diagnosis of Lyme disease, but it may have a role in diagnosis of Lyme arthritis, because it is a highly sensitive way of detecting
      <i>
       ospA
      </i>
      DNA in synovial fluid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8272083_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid8272083-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With the exception of culture or PCR, no practical means for detecting the presence of the organism is currently available, as serologic studies only test for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">
       antibodies
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      . High
      <a href="/wiki/Titer" title="Titer">
       titers
      </a>
      of either immunoglobulin G (IgG) or immunoglobulin M (IgM) antibodies to
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      antigens indicate disease, but lower titers can be misleading, because the IgM antibodies may remain after the initial infection, and IgG antibodies may remain for years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burdash_1991_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Burdash_1991-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Western blot, ELISA, and PCR can be performed by either blood test via
      <a href="/wiki/Venipuncture" title="Venipuncture">
       venipuncture
      </a>
      or cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) via
      <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_puncture" title="Lumbar puncture">
       lumbar puncture
      </a>
      . Though lumbar puncture is more definitive of diagnosis, antigen capture in the CSF is much more elusive; reportedly, CSF yields positive results in only 1030% of patients cultured. The diagnosis of neurologic infection by
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      should not be excluded solely on the basis of normal routine CSF or negative CSF antibody analyses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coyle_1995_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coyle_1995-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      New techniques for clinical testing of
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      infection have been developed, such as LTT-
      <a href="/wiki/MELISA" title="MELISA">
       MELISA
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentine_2007_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Valentine_2007-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although the results of studies are contradictory, The first peer reviewed study assessing the diagnostic sensitivity and specificity of the test has been presented in 2012, showing potential for LTT to become a supportive diagnostic tool.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23091571_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid23091571-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2014, research of LTT-MELISA concluded that it is "sensible" to include the LTT test in the diagnostic protocol for putative European-acquired Lyme borreliosis infections.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monro_2014_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Monro_2014-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Others, such as focus floating microscopy, are under investigation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisendle_2007_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eisendle_2007-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      New research indicates
      <a href="/wiki/Chemokine" title="Chemokine">
       chemokine
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/CXCL13" title="CXCL13">
       CXCL13
      </a>
      may also be a possible marker for neuroborreliosis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cadavid_2006_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cadavid_2006-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some laboratories offer Lyme disease testing using assays whose accuracy and clinical usefulness have not been adequately established. These tests include urine antigen tests, PCR tests on urine, immunofluorescent staining for cell wall-deficient forms of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      , and lymphocyte transformation tests. The CDC does not recommend these tests, and stated their use is "of great concern and is strongly discouraged".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16020686_101-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16020686-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Imaging">
       Imaging
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Imaging">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Neuroimaging" title="Neuroimaging">
       Neuroimaging
      </a>
      is controversial in whether it provides specific patterns unique to
      <a href="/wiki/Neuroborreliosis" title="Neuroborreliosis">
       neuroborreliosis
      </a>
      , but may aid in
      <a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">
       differential diagnosis
      </a>
      and in understanding the pathophysiology of the disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hildenbrand2009_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hildenbrand2009-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though controversial, some evidence shows certain neuroimaging tests can provide data that are helpful in the diagnosis of a patient.
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">
       Magnetic resonance imaging
      </a>
      (MRI) and
      <a href="/wiki/Single-photon_emission_computed_tomography" title="Single-photon emission computed tomography">
       single-photon emission computed tomography
      </a>
      (SPECT) are two of the tests that can identify abnormalities in the brain of a patient affected with this disease. Neuroimaging findings in an MRI include lesions in the periventricular white matter, as well as enlarged ventricles and cortical atrophy. The findings are considered somewhat unexceptional because the lesions have been found to be reversible following antibiotic treatment. Images produced using SPECT show numerous areas where an insufficient amount of blood is being delivered the cortex and subcortical white matter. However, SPECT images are known to be not specific because they show a heterogeneous pattern in the imaging. The abnormalities seen in the SPECT images are very similar to those seen in patients with cerebral vacuities and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creutzfeldt-Jakob_disease" title="Creutzfeldt-Jakob disease">
       Creutzfeldt-Jakob disease
      </a>
      , which makes them questionable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">
       Prevention
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Prevention">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Protective clothing includes a hat, long-sleeved shirt, and long trousers tucked into socks or boots. Light-colored clothing makes the tick more easily visible before it attaches itself. People should use special care in handling and allowing outdoor pets inside homes because they can bring ticks into the house.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Permethrin" title="Permethrin">
       Permethrin
      </a>
      sprayed on clothing kills ticks on contact, and is sold for this purpose. Insect repellents with Picaridin, IR3535, DEET, or oil of lemon eucalyptus repel ticks, as well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A community can reduce the incidence of Lyme disease by reducing the numbers of primary hosts on which the deer tick depends, such as rodents, other small mammals, and deer. Reduction of the deer population may, over time, help break the reproductive cycle of the deer ticks and their ability to flourish in suburban and rural areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulletin_2013_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bulletin_2013-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Management_of_host_animals">
       Management of host animals
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Management of host animals">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Lyme and all other deer tick-borne diseases can be prevented on a regional level by reducing the deer population on which the ticks depend for reproductive success. (Although deer ticks do acquire Lyme disease pathogens from rodents and not from deer, the size of the tick population tends to parallel that of the deer population.)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westport_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Westport-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has been demonstrated in the communities of
      <a href="/wiki/Monhegan,_Maine" title="Monhegan, Maine">
       Monhegan, Maine
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rand_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rand-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Mumford Cove, Connecticut.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-managing_deer_2007_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-managing_deer_2007-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      For example, in the U.S., reducing the deer population to levels of 8 to 10 per square mile (from the current levels of 60 or more deer per square mile in the areas of the country with the highest Lyme disease rates), the tick numbers can be brought down to levels too low to spread Lyme and other tick-borne diseases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirby_2004_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kirby_2004-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, such a drastic reduction may be impractical in many areas. Routine veterinary control of
      <a href="/wiki/Ticks_of_domestic_animals" title="Ticks of domestic animals">
       ticks of domestic animals
      </a>
      , including livestock, by use of chemical acaricides can contribute to reducing exposure of humans to ticks. However, the risk of acquiring Lyme disease does not depend on the existence of a local deer population, as is commonly assumed. Eliminating deer from smaller areas 2.5ha (6.2 acres) may in fact lead to an increase in tick density and the rise of "tick-borne disease hotspots".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perkins_117-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Perkins-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Action can be taken to avoid getting bitten by ticks by using insect repellants, for example those that contain
      <a href="/wiki/DEET" title="DEET">
       DEET
      </a>
      . DEET-containing repellants are thought to be moderately effective in the prevention of tick bites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11929056_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11929056-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Europe known reservoirs of
      <i>
       Borrelia burgdorferi
      </i>
      were 9 small mammals, 7 medium-sized mammals and 16 species of birds (including passerines, sea-birds and pheasants).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gern1998_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gern1998-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These animals seem to transmit spirochetes to ticks and thus participate in the natural circulation of B. burgdorferi in Europe. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus_musculus" title="Mus musculus">
       house mouse
      </a>
      is also suspected as well as other species of small rodents, particularly in Eastern Europe and Russia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gern1998_119-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gern1998-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <br/>
      "The reservoir species that contain the most pathogens are the European roe deer
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capreolus_capreolus" title="Capreolus capreolus">
        Capreolus capreolus
       </a>
      </i>
      ;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wodecka2013_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wodecka2013-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "
      <i>
       it does not appear to serve as a major reservoir of B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      " thought Jaenson &amp; al. (1992)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (incompetent host for
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      and TBE virus) but it is important for feeding the ticks,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as
      <a href="/wiki/Red_deer" title="Red deer">
       red deer
      </a>
      and wild
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boars" title="Boars">
       boars
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sus_scrofa" title="Sus scrofa">
        Sus scrofa
       </a>
      </i>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wodecka2014_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wodecka2014-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in which one
      <i>
       Rickettsia
      </i>
      and three
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      species were identified",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wodecka2013_120-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wodecka2013-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with high risks of coinfection in roe deer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, in the 2000s, in roe deer in Europe "
      <i>
       two species of Rickettsia and two species of Borrelia were identified
      </i>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wodecka2014_123-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wodecka2014-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccination">
       Vaccination
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Vaccination">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">
       recombinant vaccine
      </a>
      against Lyme disease, based on the outer surface protein A (ospA) of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      , was developed by SmithKline Beecham. In
      <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">
       clinical trials
      </a>
      involving more than 10,000 people, the vaccine, called LYMErix, was found to confer protective immunity to
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      in 76% of adults and 100% of children with only mild or moderate and transient
      <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">
       adverse effects
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poland_2001_125-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Poland_2001-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      LYMErix was approved on the basis of these trials by the
      <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">
       Food and Drug Administration
      </a>
      (FDA) on December 21, 1998.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following approval of the vaccine, its entry in clinical practice was slow for a variety of reasons, including its cost, which was often not reimbursed by insurance companies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rowe_199_126-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rowe_199-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Subsequently, hundreds of vaccine recipients reported they had developed
      <a href="/wiki/Autoimmunity" title="Autoimmunity">
       autoimmune
      </a>
      side effects. Supported by some patient advocacy groups, a number of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class-action_lawsuit" title="Class-action lawsuit">
       class-action lawsuits
      </a>
      were filed against GlaxoSmithKline, alleging the vaccine had caused these health problems. These claims were investigated by the FDA and the Centers for Disease Control, which found no connection between the vaccine and the autoimmune complaints.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uphill_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-uphill-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite the lack of evidence that the complaints were caused by the vaccine, sales plummeted and LYMErix was withdrawn from the U.S. market by GlaxoSmithKline in February 2002,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaccine_2008_128-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vaccine_2008-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in the setting of negative media coverage and fears of vaccine side effects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uphill_127-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-uphill-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cautionary_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cautionary-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The fate of LYMErix was described in the medical literature as a "cautionary tale";
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cautionary_129-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cautionary-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      an editorial in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">
        Nature
       </a>
      </i>
      cited the withdrawal of LYMErix as an instance in which "unfounded public fears place pressures on vaccine developers that go beyond reasonable safety considerations."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-safe_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-safe-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The original developer of the OspA vaccine at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute" title="Max Planck Institute">
       Max Planck Institute
      </a>
      told
      <i>
       Nature
      </i>
      : "This just shows how irrational the world can be... There was no scientific justification for the first OspA vaccine LYMErix being pulled."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uphill_127-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-uphill-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      New vaccines are being researched using outer surface protein C (OspC) and
      <a href="/wiki/Glycolipid" title="Glycolipid">
       glycolipoprotein
      </a>
      as methods of immunization.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earnhart_2007_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Earnhart_2007-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pozsgay_2007_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pozsgay_2007-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vaccines have been formulated and approved for prevention of Lyme disease in dogs. Currently, three Lyme disease vaccines are available. LymeVax, formulated by Fort Dodge Laboratories, contains intact dead spirochetes which expose the host to the organism. Galaxy Lyme, Intervet-
      <a href="/wiki/Schering-Plough" title="Schering-Plough">
       Schering-Plough
      </a>
      's vaccine, targets proteins OspC and OspA. The OspC antibodies kill any of the bacteria that have not been killed by the OspA antibodies. Canine Recombinant Lyme, formulated by
      <a href="/wiki/Merial" title="Merial">
       Merial
      </a>
      , generates antibodies against the OspA protein so a tick feeding on a vaccinated dog draws in blood full of anti-OspA antibodies, which kill the spirochetes in the tick's gut before they are transmitted to the dog.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks.2C_DVM_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brooks.2C_DVM-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tick_removal">
       Tick removal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Tick removal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Attached ticks should be removed promptly, as removal within 36 hours can reduce transmission rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piesman2002_134-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-piesman2002-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Folk remedies for tick removal tend to be ineffective, offer no advantages in preventing the transfer of disease, and may increase the risks of transmission or infection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The best method is simply to pull the tick out with tweezers as close to the skin as possible, without twisting, and avoiding crushing the body of the tick or removing the head from the tick's body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA_2007_136-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JAMA_2007-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The risk of infection increases with the time the tick is attached, and if a tick is attached for less than 24 hours, infection is unlikely. However, since these ticks are very small, especially in the nymph stage, prompt detection is quite difficult.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piesman2002_134-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-piesman2002-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Australian Society of Clinical Immunology recommends against using tweezers to remove ticks but rather to kill the tick first by using a product to rapidly freeze the tick to prevent it from injecting more allergen-containing saliva. In a tick allergic person, the tick should be killed and removed in a safe place (e.g. an emergency department of a hospital).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Preventive_antibiotics">
       Preventive antibiotics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Preventive antibiotics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The risk of infectious transmission increases with the duration of tick attachment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It requires between 36 and 48 hours of attachment for the bacteria that causes Lyme to travel from within the tick into its saliva.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If a deer tick that is sufficiently likely to be carrying
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      is found attached to a person and removed, and if the tick has been attached for 36 hours or is engorged, a single dose of doxycycline administered within the 72 hours after removal may reduce the risk of Lyme disease. It is not generally recommended as development of infection is rare: about 50 people would have to be treated this way to prevent one case of infection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">
       Treatment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Treatment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tickremoval.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="547" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Tickremoval.png/220px-Tickremoval.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Tickremoval.png/330px-Tickremoval.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Tickremoval.png/440px-Tickremoval.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tickremoval.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Removal of a tick using tweezers
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">
       Antibiotics
      </a>
      are the primary treatment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2014_1-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEJM2014-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The specific approach to their use is dependent on the individual affected and the stage of the disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For most people with early localized infection, oral administration of
      <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">
       doxycycline
      </a>
      is widely recommended as the first choice, as it is effective against not only
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      bacteria but also a variety of other illnesses carried by ticks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Doxycycline is contraindicated in children younger than eight years of age and women who are pregnant or breastfeeding;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      alternatives to doxycycline are amoxicillin, cefuroxime axetil, and
      <a href="/wiki/Azithromycin" title="Azithromycin">
       azithromycin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Individuals with early disseminated or late infection may have symptomatic cardiac disease, refractory Lyme arthritis, or neurologic symptoms like meningitis or encephalitis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Intravenous administration of
      <a href="/wiki/Ceftriaxone" title="Ceftriaxone">
       ceftriaxone
      </a>
      is recommended as the first choice in these cases;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Cefotaxime" title="Cefotaxime">
       cefotaxime
      </a>
      and doxycycline are available as alternatives.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      These treatment regimens last from one to four weeks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If joint swelling persists or returns, a second round of antibiotics may be considered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Outside of that, a prolonged antibiotic regimen lasting more than 28 days is not recommended as no clinical evidence shows it to be effective.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      IgM and IgG antibody levels may be elevated for years even after successful treatment with antibiotics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As antibody levels are not indicative of treatment success, testing for them is not recommended.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_2012_138-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-wright_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">
       Prognosis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Prognosis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      For early cases, prompt
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness">
         <span title="Wikipedia:Vagueness">
          Specific Time?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      treatment is usually curative.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krause_2006_139-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Krause_2006-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the severity and treatment of Lyme disease may be complicated due to late diagnosis, failure of antibiotic treatment, and simultaneous infection with other tick-borne diseases (coinfections), including
      <a href="/wiki/Ehrlichiosis" title="Ehrlichiosis">
       ehrlichiosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Babesiosis" title="Babesiosis">
       babesiosis
      </a>
      , and immune suppression
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      in the patient.
     </p>
     <p>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">
       meta-analysis
      </a>
      published in 2005 found some patients with Lyme disease have fatigue, joint or muscle pain, and
      <a href="/wiki/Neurocognitive" title="Neurocognitive">
       neurocognitive
      </a>
      symptoms persisting for years, despite antibiotic treatment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cairns_140-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cairns-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Patients with late stage Lyme disease have been shown to experience a level of physical
      <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">
       disability
      </a>
      equivalent to that seen in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" title="Congestive heart failure">
       congestive heart failure
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klempner_141-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Klempner-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In dogs, a serious long-term prognosis may result in glomerular disease,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck_2012_142-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-merck_2012-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which is a category of kidney damage that may cause chronic kidney disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks.2C_DVM_133-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brooks.2C_DVM-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dogs may also experience chronic joint disease if the disease is left untreated. However, the majority of cases of Lyme disease in dogs result in a complete recovery with, and sometimes without, treatment with antibiotics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-staubinger_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-staubinger-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
         <span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (July 2013)">
          verification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      In rare cases, Lyme disease can be fatal to both humans and dogs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fatal-cases_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fatal-cases-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">
       Epidemiology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png/300px-Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png/450px-Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png/600px-Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geographical_distribution_of_reported_Lyme_Disease_cases.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Countries with reported Lyme disease cases.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Lyme disease
      <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">
       occurs regularly
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">
       Northern Hemisphere
      </a>
      temperate regions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higgins_2004_145-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Higgins_2004-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">
       Africa
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Africa">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In northern Africa,
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      has been identified in
      <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
       Morocco
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">
       Algeria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">
       Tunisia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouattour_2004_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bouattour_2004-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dsouli_2006_147-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dsouli_2006-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helmy_2000_148-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Helmy_2000-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lyme disease in sub-Saharan Africa is presently unknown, but evidence indicates it may occur in humans in this region. The abundance of hosts and tick vectors would favor the establishment of Lyme infection in Africa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fivaz_1989_149-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fivaz_1989-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In East Africa, two cases of Lyme disease have been reported in
      <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">
       Kenya
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jowi_2005_150-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jowi_2005-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">
       Asia
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Asia">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      -infested ticks are being found more frequently in
      <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
       Japan
      </a>
      , as well as in northwest
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">
       Nepal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
       Thailand
      </a>
      and far eastern
      <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
       Russia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_1998_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Li_1998-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15623946_152-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15623946-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      has also been isolated in
      <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">
       Mongolia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walder_2006_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Walder_2006-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">
       Europe
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Europe">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In Europe, Lyme disease is caused by infection with one or more pathogenic European genospecies of the spirochaete
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      , mainly transmitted by the tick
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_ricinus" title="Ixodes ricinus">
        Ixodes ricinus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21794218_154-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid21794218-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cases of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      -infected ticks are found predominantly in central Europe, particularly in
      <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
       Slovenia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
       Austria
      </a>
      , but have been isolated in almost every country on the continent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16819127_155-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16819127-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Incidence in southern Europe, such as Italy and Portugal, is much lower.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17130658_156-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17130658-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">
       United Kingdom
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       United Kingdom
      </a>
      the number of laboratory confirmed cases of Lyme disease has been rising steadily since voluntary reporting was introduced in 1986
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpaEpidemiology_157-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpaEpidemiology-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      when 68 cases were recorded in the UK and
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">
       Republic of Ireland
      </a>
      combined.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muhlemann_1987_158-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Muhlemann_1987-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the UK there were 23 confirmed cases in 1988 and 19 in 1990,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard1991_159-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hansard1991-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but 973 in 2009
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpaEpidemiology_157-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpaEpidemiology-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and 953 in 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpa5-15_160-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpa5-15-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Provisional figures for the first 3 quarters of 2011 show a 26% increase on the same period in 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disease_action_2011_161-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-disease_action_2011-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is thought, however, that the actual number of cases is significantly higher than suggested by the above figures, with the UK's
      <a href="/wiki/Health_Protection_Agency" title="Health Protection Agency">
       Health Protection Agency
      </a>
      estimating that there are between 2,000 and 3,000 cases per year,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpa5-15_160-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpa5-15-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (with an average of around 15% of the infections acquired overseas
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpaEpidemiology_157-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpaEpidemiology-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ), while Dr Darrel Ho-Yen, Director of the Scottish Toxoplasma Reference Laboratory and National Lyme Disease Testing Service, believes that the number of confirmed cases should be multiplied by 10 "to take account of wrongly diagnosed cases, tests giving false results, sufferers who weren't tested, people who are infected but not showing symptoms, failures to notify and infected individuals who don't consult a doctor."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassidy_2011_162-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cassidy_2011-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-danger_2009_163-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-danger_2009-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite Lyme disease (Borrelia burgdorferi infection) being a
      <a href="/wiki/Notifiable_disease" title="Notifiable disease">
       notifiable disease
      </a>
      in Scotland
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scotland_2012_164-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-scotland_2012-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      since January 1990
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard_1997_165-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hansard_1997-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which should therefore be reported on the basis of clinical suspicion, it is believed that many
      <a href="/wiki/General_practitioner" title="General practitioner">
       GPs
      </a>
      are unaware of the requirement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fox_2010_166-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fox_2010-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mandatory reporting, limited to laboratory test results only, was introduced throughout the UK in October 2010, under the Health Protection (Notification) Regulations 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpaEpidemiology_157-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpaEpidemiology-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although there is a greater incidence of Lyme disease in the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Forest" title="New Forest">
       New Forest
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Plain" title="Salisbury Plain">
       Salisbury Plain
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Exmoor" title="Exmoor">
       Exmoor
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/South_Downs" title="South Downs">
       South Downs
      </a>
      , parts of
      <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire" title="Wiltshire">
       Wiltshire
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Berkshire" title="Berkshire">
       Berkshire
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thetford_Forest" title="Thetford Forest">
       Thetford Forest
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpa_2009_167-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hpa_2009-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the West coast and islands of
      <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
       Scotland
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defra_2009_168-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-defra_2009-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      infected ticks are widespread, and can even be found in the parks of
      <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
       London
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard1991_159-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hansard1991-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tick_bite_169-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-tick_bite-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 1989 report found that 25% of forestry workers in the New Forest were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seropositive" title="Seropositive">
       seropositive
      </a>
      , as were between 2% and 4-5% of the general local population of the area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8037992_170-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid8037992-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2563850_171-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2563850-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tests on pet dogs, carried out throughout the country in 2009 indicated that around 2.5% of ticks in the UK may be infected, considerably higher than previously thought.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_2012_172-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith_2012-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_dogs_2012_173-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbc_dogs_2012-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is thought that
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       global warming
      </a>
      may lead to an increase in tick activity in the future, as well as an increase in the amount of time that people spend in public parks, thus increasing the risk of infection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-londong_2008_174-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-londong_2008-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">
       North America
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: North America">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Many studies in North America have examined ecological and environmental correlates of Lyme disease prevalence. A 2005 study using climate suitability modelling of
      <i>
       I. scapularis
      </i>
      projected that
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">
       climate change
      </a>
      would cause an overall 213% increase in suitable vector habitat by the year 2080, with northward expansions in Canada, increased suitability in the central U.S., and decreased suitable habitat and vector retraction in the southern U.S.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brownstein_etal_2005_175-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brownstein_etal_2005-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2008 review of published studies concluded that the presence of forests or forested areas was the only variable that consistently elevated the risk of Lyme disease, and that other environmental variables showed little or no concordance between studies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Killilea_etal_2008_176-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Killilea_etal_2008-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The authors argued that the factors influencing tick density and human risk between sites are still poorly understood, and that future studies should be conducted over longer time periods, become more standardized across regions, and incorporate existing knowledge of regional Lyme disease ecology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Killilea_etal_2008_176-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Killilea_etal_2008-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">
       Canada
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Canada">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Owing to changing climate, the range of ticks able to carry Lyme disease has expanded from a limited area of Ontario to include areas of southern Quebec, Manitoba, northern Ontario, southern New Brunswick, south-west Nova Scotia and limited parts of Saskatchewan and Alberta, as well as British Columbia. Cases have been reported as far east as the island of Newfoundland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-min_ag_2012_177-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-min_ag_2012-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_sheet_2012_178-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fact_sheet_2012-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogden_2009_179-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ogden_2009-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A model-based prediction by Leighton
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2012) suggests that the range of the
      <i>
       I. scapularis
      </i>
      tick will expand into Canada by 46km/year over the next decade, with warming climatic temperatures as the main driver of increased speed of spread.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leighton_2012_180-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leighton_2012-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">
       Mexico
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mexico">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A 2007 study suggests
      <i>
       Borrelia burgdorferi
      </i>
      infections are endemic to Mexico, from four cases reported between 1999 and 2000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_dispatch_oct_2007_181-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc_dispatch_oct_2007-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
       United States
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: United States">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="556" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif/300px-Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif/450px-Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lyme_Disease_Risk_Map.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">
         CDC
        </a>
        map showing the risk of Lyme disease in the United States, particularly its concentration in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_Megalopolis" title="Northeast Megalopolis">
         Northeast Megalopolis
        </a>
        and western
        <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">
         Wisconsin
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Each year, approximately 30,000 new cases are reported to the CDC however, this number is likely underestimated. The CDC is currently conducting research on evaluation and diagnostics of the disease and preliminary results suggest the number of new cases to be around 300,000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lyme disease is the most common tick-borne disease in North America and Europe, and one of the fastest-growing infectious diseases in the United States. Of cases reported to the United States CDC, the ratio of Lyme disease infection is 7.9 cases for every 100,000 persons. In the ten states where Lyme disease is most common, the average was 31.6 cases for every 100,000 persons for the year 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_stats_2012_183-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc_stats_2012-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmwr_2007_184-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mmwr_2007-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bacon_2008_185-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bacon_2008-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although Lyme disease has been reported in all states except Montana,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_186-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      about 99% of all reported cases are confined to just five geographic areas (New England, Mid-Atlantic, East-North Central, South Atlantic, and West North-Central).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_stats2_187-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc_stats2-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      New 2011 CDC Lyme case definition guidelines are used to determine confirmed CDC surveillance cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_surveil_current_188-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc_surveil_current-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Effective January 2008, the CDC gives equal weight to laboratory evidence from 1) a positive culture for
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      ; 2) two-tier testing (
      <a href="/wiki/ELISA" title="ELISA">
       ELISA
      </a>
      screening and
      <a href="/wiki/Western_blot" title="Western blot">
       Western blot
      </a>
      confirming); or 3) single-tier
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgG" title="IgG">
       IgG
      </a>
      (old infection) Western blot.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_surveil_2008_189-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc_surveil_2008-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Previously, the CDC only included laboratory evidence based on (1) and (2) in their surveillance case definition. The case definition now includes the use of Western blot without prior ELISA screen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_surveil_2008_189-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdc_surveil_2008-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The number of reported cases of the disease has been increasing, as are endemic regions in North America. For example,
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      was previously thought to be hindered in its ability to be maintained in an
      <a href="/wiki/Enzootic" title="Enzootic">
       enzootic
      </a>
      cycle in
      <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
       California
      </a>
      , because it was assumed the large lizard population would dilute the prevalence of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      in local tick populations; this has since been brought into question, as some evidence has suggested lizards can become infected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17417956_190-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17417956-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Except for one study in Europe,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16820453_191-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16820453-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      much of the data implicating lizards is based on DNA detection of the spirochete and has not demonstrated lizards are able to infect ticks feeding upon them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17417956_190-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17417956-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17626342_192-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17626342-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17427701_193-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17427701-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17326941_194-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17326941-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As some experiments suggest lizards are refractory to infection with
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      , it appears likely their involvement in the enzootic cycle is more complex and species-specific.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16995383_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16995383-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      is most associated with ticks hosted by
      <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_deer" title="White-tailed deer">
       white-tailed deer
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White-footed_Mouse" title="White-footed Mouse">
       white-footed mice
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Borrelia afzelii
      </i>
      is most frequently detected in rodent-feeding vector ticks, and
      <i>
       Borrelia garinii
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Borrelia valaisiana
      </i>
      appear to be associated with birds. Both rodents and birds are competent reservoir hosts for
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu stricto
      </i>
      . The resistance of a genospecies of Lyme disease spirochetes to the bacteriolytic activities of the alternative complement pathway of various host species may determine its reservoir host association.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Several similar but apparently distinct conditions may exist, caused by various species or subspecies of
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      in North America. A regionally restricted condition that may be related to
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      infection is
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_tick-associated_rash_illness" title="Southern tick-associated rash illness">
       southern tick-associated rash illness
      </a>
      (STARI), also known as Masters' disease.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amblyomma_americanum" title="Amblyomma americanum">
        Amblyomma americanum
       </a>
      </i>
      , known commonly as the lone-star tick, is recognized as the primary vector for STARI. In some parts of the geographical distribution of STARI, Lyme disease is quite rare (e.g., Arkansas), so patients in these regions experiencing Lyme-like symptomsespecially if they follow a bite from a lone-star tickshould consider STARI as a possibility. It is generally a milder condition than Lyme and typically responds well to antibiotic treatment.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although Montana is the only state that has not reported a confirmed case of Lyme disease, in recent years there have been 5 to 10 cases a year of a disease similar to Lyme. It occurs primarily in pockets along the
      <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_River" title="Yellowstone River">
       Yellowstone River
      </a>
      in central Montana. People have developed a red bull's-eye rash around a tick bite followed by weeks of fatigue and a fever.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_186-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lyme disease prevalence is comparable among males and females. A wide range of age groups is affected, though the number of cases is highest among 1019-year-olds. For unknown reasons, Lyme disease is seven times more common among Asians.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vox_facts_195-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vox_facts-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">
       South America
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: South America">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
       South America
      </a>
      , tick-borne disease recognition and occurrence is rising. Ticks carrying
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      , as well as canine and human tick-borne disease, have been reported widely in
      <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
       Brazil
      </a>
      , but the subspecies of
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      has not yet been defined.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mantovani_2007_196-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mantovani_2007-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first reported case of Lyme disease in Brazil was made in 1993 in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sao_Paulo" title="Sao Paulo">
       Sao Paulo
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshinari_1993_197-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yoshinari_1993-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu stricto
      </i>
      antigens in patients have been identified in
      <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">
       Colombia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">
       Bolivia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The evolutionary history of
      <i>
       Borrelia burgdorferi
      </i>
      genetics has been the subject of recent studies. One study has found that prior to the
      <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">
       reforestation
      </a>
