      that accompanied post colonial farm abandonment in
      <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">
       New England
      </a>
      and the wholesale migration into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-west" title="Mid-west">
       mid-west
      </a>
      that occurred during the early 19th century, Lyme disease was present for thousands of years in America and had spread along with its tick hosts from the Northeast to the Midwest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoen_2009_198-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoen_2009-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      John Josselyn, who visited New England in 1638 and again from 16631670, wrote "there be infinite numbers of tikes hanging upon the bushes in summer time that will cleave to man's garments and creep into his breeches eating themselves in a short time into the very flesh of a man. I have seen the stockins of those that have gone through the woods covered with them."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josselyn_1670_199-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Josselyn_1670-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This is also confirmed by the writings of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Kalm" title="Peter Kalm">
       Peter Kalm
      </a>
      , a Swedish botanist who was sent to America by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnaeus" title="Linnaeus">
       Linnaeus
      </a>
      , and who found the forests of New York "abound" with ticks when he visited in 1749. When Kalm's journey was retraced 100 years later, the forests were gone and the Lyme bacterium had probably become isolated to a few pockets along the northeast coast, Wisconsin, and Minnesota.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drymon_2008_200-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Drymon_2008-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Perhaps the first detailed description of what is now known as Lyme disease appeared in the writings of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverend_Dr_John_Walker" title="Reverend Dr John Walker">
       Reverend Dr John Walker
      </a>
      after a visit to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_of_Jura" title="Island of Jura">
       Island of Jura
      </a>
      (Deer Island) off the west coast of Scotland in 1764.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summerton_1995_201-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-summerton_1995-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He gives a good description both of the symptoms of Lyme disease (with "exquisite pain [in] the interior parts of the limbs") and of the tick vector itself, which he describes as a "worm" with a body which is "of a reddish colour and of a compressed shape with a row of feet on each side" that "penetrates the skin". Many people from this area of Great Britain immigrated to North America between 1717 and the end of the 18th century.
     </p>
     <p>
      The examination of preserved museum specimens has found
      <i>
       Borrelia
      </i>
      DNA in an infected
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ixodes_ricinus" title="Ixodes ricinus">
        Ixodes ricinus
       </a>
      </i>
      tick from Germany that dates back to 1884, and from an infected mouse from Cape Cod that died in 1894.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drymon_2008_200-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Drymon_2008-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 2010 autopsy of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" title="tzi the Iceman">
       tzi the Iceman
      </a>
      , a 5,300-year-old
      <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">
       mummy
      </a>
      , revealed the presence of the DNA sequence of
      <i>
       Borrelia burgdorferi
      </i>
      making him the earliest known human with Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngautopsy_202-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ngautopsy-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The early European studies of what is now known as Lyme disease described its skin manifestations. The first study dates to 1883 in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breslau" title="Breslau">
       Breslau
      </a>
      , Germany (now
      <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocaw">
       Wrocaw
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
       Poland
      </a>
      ), where physician Alfred Buchwald described a man who had suffered for 16 years with a degenerative
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_disorder" title="Skin disorder">
       skin disorder
      </a>
      now known as
      <a href="/wiki/Acrodermatitis_chronica_atrophicans" title="Acrodermatitis chronica atrophicans">
       acrodermatitis chronica atrophicans
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afzelius1910_203-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Afzelius1910-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">
       20th century
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 20th century">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      At a 1909 research conference, Swedish dermatologist
      <a href="/wiki/Arvid_Afzelius" title="Arvid Afzelius">
       Arvid Afzelius
      </a>
      presented a study about an expanding, ring-like lesion he had observed in an older woman following the bite of a sheep tick. He named the lesion
      <i>
       erythema migrans
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afzelius1910_203-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Afzelius1910-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The skin condition now known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borrelial_lymphocytoma" title="Borrelial lymphocytoma">
       borrelial lymphocytoma
      </a>
      was first described in 1911.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burckhardt_1911_204-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-burckhardt_1911-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Neurological problems following tick bites were recognized starting in the 1920s. French physicians Garin and Bujadoux described a farmer with a painful sensory
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiculitis" title="Radiculitis">
       radiculitis
      </a>
      accompanied by mild
      <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
       meningitis
      </a>
      following a tick bite. A large, ring-shaped rash was also noted, although the doctors did not relate it to the meningoradiculitis. In 1930, the Swedish dermatologist Sven Hellerstrm was the first to propose EM and neurological symptoms following a tick bite were related.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hellerstrom_1930_205-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hellerstrom_1930-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 1940s, German neurologist
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Bannwarth" title="Alfred Bannwarth">
       Alfred Bannwarth
      </a>
      described several cases of chronic lymphocytic meningitis and polyradiculoneuritis, some of which were accompanied by erythematous skin lesions.
     </p>
     <p>
      Carl Lennhoff, who worked at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karolinska_Institute" title="Karolinska Institute">
       Karolinska Institute
      </a>
      in Sweden, believed many skin conditions were caused by spirochetes. In 1948, he used a special stain to microscopically observe what he believed were spirochetes in various types of skin lesions, including EM.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lenhoff_1948_206-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lenhoff_1948-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        206
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although his conclusions were later shown to be erroneous, interest in the study of spirochetes was sparked. In 1949, Nils Thyresson, who also worked at the Karolinska Institute, was the first to treat ACA with penicillin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18140373_207-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18140373-207">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        207
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 1950s, the relationship among tick bite, lymphocytoma, EM and Bannwarth's syndrome was recognized throughout Europe leading to the widespread use of
      <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">
       penicillin
      </a>
      for treatment in Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bianchi_1950_208-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bianchi_1950-208">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        208
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paschoud_1954_209-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Paschoud_1954-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1970, a dermatologist in
      <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">
       Wisconsin
      </a>
      named Rudolph Scrimenti recognized an EM lesion in a patient after recalling a paper by Hellerstrm that had been reprinted in an American science journal in 1950. This was the first documented case of EM in the United States. Based on the European literature, he treated the patient with penicillin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scrimenti_1970_210-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scrimenti_1970-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The full
      <a href="/wiki/Syndrome" title="Syndrome">
       syndrome
      </a>
      now known as Lyme disease was not recognized until a cluster of cases originally thought to be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juvenile_rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Juvenile rheumatoid arthritis">
       juvenile rheumatoid arthritis
      </a>
      was identified in three towns in southeastern
      <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">
       Connecticut
      </a>
      in 1975, including the towns
      <a href="/wiki/Lyme,_Connecticut" title="Lyme, Connecticut">
       Lyme
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Lyme" title="Old Lyme">
       Old Lyme
      </a>
      , which gave the disease its popular name.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchers2015_211-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Borchers2015-211">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        211
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was investigated by physicians David Snydman and
      <a href="/wiki/Allen_Steere" title="Allen Steere">
       Allen Steere
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Epidemic_Intelligence_Service" title="Epidemic Intelligence Service">
       Epidemic Intelligence Service
      </a>
      , and by others from
      <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">
       Yale University
      </a>
      , including Dr.
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Malawista" title="Stephen Malawista">
       Stephen Malawista
      </a>
      , who is credited as a co-discover of the disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hcourant_212-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hcourant-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The recognition that the patients in the United States had EM led to the recognition that "Lyme arthritis" was one manifestation of the same tick-borne condition known in Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sternbach_213-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sternbach-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Before 1976, elements of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi sensu lato
      </i>
      infection were called or known as tick-borne meningopolyneuritis, Garin-Bujadoux syndrome, Bannwarth syndrome, Afzelius' disease,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bolognia_214-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bolognia-214">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Montauk,_New_York" title="Montauk, New York">
       Montauk Knee
      </a>
      or sheep tick fever. Since 1976 the disease is most often referred to as Lyme disease,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mast_1976_215-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mast_1976-215">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        215
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steere_1977_216-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steere_1977-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lyme borreliosis or simply borreliosis.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1980, Steere,
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , began to test
      <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">
       antibiotic
      </a>
      regimens in adult patients with Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steere_1983_217-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steere_1983-217">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        217
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the same year, New York State Health Dept. epidemiologist
      <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Benach" title="Jorge Benach">
       Jorge Benach
      </a>
      provided
      <a href="/wiki/Willy_Burgdorfer" title="Willy Burgdorfer">
       Willy Burgdorfer
      </a>
      , a researcher at the
      <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_Biological_Laboratory" title="Rocky Mountain Biological Laboratory">
       Rocky Mountain Biological Laboratory
      </a>
      , with collections of
      <i>
       I. dammini
      </i>
      [
      <i>
       scapularis
      </i>
      ] from Shelter Island, NY, a known Lyme-endemic area as part of an ongoing investigation of Rocky Mountain spotted fever. In examining the ticks for rickettsiae, Burgdorfer noticed "poorly stained, rather long, irregularly coiled spirochetes." Further examination revealed spirochetes in 60% of the ticks. Burgdorfer credited his familiarity with the European literature for his realization that the spirochetes might be the "long-sought cause of ECM and Lyme disease." Benach supplied him with more ticks from Shelter Island and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_serum" title="Blood serum">
       sera
      </a>
      from patients diagnosed with Lyme disease. University of Texas Health Science Center researcher Alan Barbour "offered his expertise to culture and immunochemically characterize the organism." Burgdorfer subsequently confirmed his discovery by isolating, from patients with Lyme disease, spirochetes identical to those found in ticks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorfer_1984_218-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Burgdorfer_1984-218">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        218
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 1982, he published his findings in
      <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">
       Science
      </a>
      , and the spirochete was named
      <i>
       Borrelia burgdorferi
      </i>
      in his honor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorfer_1982_219-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Burgdorfer_1982-219">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After the identification of
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      as the causative agent of Lyme disease, antibiotics were selected for testing, guided by
      <i>
       in vitro
      </i>
      antibiotic sensitivities, including
      <a href="/wiki/Tetracycline_antibiotics" title="Tetracycline antibiotics">
       tetracycline antibiotics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin" title="Amoxicillin">
       amoxicillin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cefuroxime_axetil" title="Cefuroxime axetil">
       cefuroxime axetil
      </a>
      , intravenous and intramuscular penicillin and intravenous
      <a href="/wiki/Ceftriaxone" title="Ceftriaxone">
       ceftriaxone
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luft_1988_220-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Luft_1988-220">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        220
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dattwyler_1990_221-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dattwyler_1990-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mechanism of tick transmission was also the subject of much discussion.
      <i>
       B. burgdorferi
      </i>
      spirochetes were identified in tick saliva in 1987, confirming the hypothesis that transmission occurred via tick salivary glands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribeiro_1987_222-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ribeiro_1987-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Jonathan Edlow, Professor of Medicine at Harvard Medical School, quotes the late Ed Masters (discoverer of
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_tick-associated_rash_illness" title="Southern tick-associated rash illness">
       STARI
      </a>
      , a Lyme-like illness) in his book
      <i>
       Bull's-Eye
      </i>
      , on the history of Lyme disease. Edlow writes:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Masters points out that the "track record" of the "conventional wisdom" regarding Lyme disease is not very good: "First off, they said it was a new disease, which it wasn't. Then it was thought to be viral, but it isn't. Then it was thought that sero-negativity didn't exist, which it does. They thought it was easily treated by short courses of antibiotics, which sometimes it isn't. Then it was only the
       <i>
        Ixodes dammini
       </i>
       tick, which we now know is not even a separate valid tick species. If you look throughout the history, almost every time a major dogmatic statement has been made about what we 'know' about this disease, it was subsequently proven wrong or underwent major modifications."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edlow_2003_223-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Edlow_2003-223">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         223
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">
       Society and culture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Society and culture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">
       Urbanization
      </a>
      and other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" title="Anthropogenic">
       anthropogenic
      </a>
      factors can be implicated in the spread of Lyme disease to humans. In many areas, expansion of suburban neighborhoods has led to gradual deforestation of surrounding wooded areas and increased border contact between humans and tick-dense areas. Human expansion has also resulted in reduction of predators that hunt deer as well as mice, chipmunks and other small rodentsthe primary reservoirs for Lyme disease. As a consequence of increased human contact with host and
      <a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">
       vector
      </a>
      , the likelihood of transmission of the disease has greatly increased.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoGiudice_224-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LoGiudice-224">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        224
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patz_225-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patz-225">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Researchers are investigating possible links between
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       global warming
      </a>
      and the spread of vector-borne diseases, including Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khasnis_226-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Khasnis-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        226
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_over_term_.22chronic_Lyme_disease.22">
       <span id="CHRONIC">
       </span>
       Controversy over term "chronic Lyme disease"
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title='Edit section: Controversy over term "chronic Lyme disease"'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease_controversy" title="Lyme disease controversy">
       Lyme disease controversy
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The term "chronic Lyme disease" is controversial and not recognized in the medical literature,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nejm-feder_227-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nejm-feder-227">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        227
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and most medical authorities advise against long-term antibiotic treatment for Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idsa_guideline_93-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-idsa_guideline-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17522387_228-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17522387-228">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        228
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nih-cld_229-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nih-cld-229">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        229
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies have shown that most patients diagnosed with "chronic Lyme disease" either have no objective evidence of previous or current infection with
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B._burgdorferi" title="B. burgdorferi">
        B. burgdorferi
       </a>
      </i>
      or are patients who should be classified as having post-treatment Lyme disease syndrome (PTLDS), which is defined as continuing or relapsing non-specific symptoms (such as fatigue, musculoskeletal pain, and cognitive complaints) in a patient previously treated for Lyme disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marques_230-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marques-230">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        230
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">
       Other animals
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lyme_disease&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Other animals">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Prevention of Lyme disease is an important step in keeping dogs safe in endemic areas. Prevention education and a number of preventative measures are available. First, for dog owners who live near or who often frequent tick-infested areas, routine vaccinations of their dogs is an important step.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20207198_231-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20207198-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        231
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another crucial preventive measure is the use of persistent acaricides, such as topical repellents or pesticides that contain triazapentadienes (
      <a href="/wiki/Amitraz" title="Amitraz">
       Amitraz
      </a>
      ), phenylpyrazoles (
      <a href="/wiki/Fipronil" title="Fipronil">
       Fipronil
      </a>
      ), or permethrin (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrethroids" title="Pyrethroids">
       pyrethroids
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20933139_232-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20933139-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These acaricides target primarily the adult stages of Lyme-carrying ticks and reduce the number of reproductively active ticks in the environment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20207198_231-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20207198-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        231
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Formulations of these ingredients are available in a variety of topical forms, including spot-ons, sprays, powders, impregnated collars, solutions, and shampoos.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20933139_232-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20933139-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Examination of a dog for ticks after being in a tick-infested area is an important precautionary measure to take in the prevention of Lyme disease. Key spots to examine include the head, neck, and ears.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hahn_233-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hahn-233">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        233
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>

       </span>
      </span>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALyme+disease&amp;rft.aufirst=Jeffrey&amp;rft.au=Hahn%2C+Jeffrey&amp;rft.aulast=Hahn&amp;rft.btitle=Ticks+and+Their+Control&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.extension.umn.edu%2Fdistribution%2Fhorticulture%2FDD1013.html&amp;rft.pub=Regents+of+the+University+of+Minnesota&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
